
Trickle-down Network EffectsOpen sidebarTrickle-down Network EffectsModel: Default (GPT-3.5)Summarize: While prior literature has documented that the incidence of migration rises with educational 
attainment (Saint-Paul, 2004; Docquier and Marfouk, 2006; Docquier and Rapoport, 2012; 
Grogger and Hanson, 2011; Kerr, et al., 2016), our work reveals that this masks considerable 
heterogeneity among the tertiary-educated in the ability dimension. Indeed, the incidence of 
migration rises dramatically among the most extraordinarily able, as conjectured by Saint-Paul 
(2004). We have also documented that graduates of the most elite IITs are more likely to migrate 
abroad after graduating compared to equally talented individuals who chose other IITs. Prior 
research has documented the large enrollment of Indian students in U.S. graduate programs 
(Bound, et al., 2021). We show that Indian educational institutions are playing an important role 
in facilitating this enrollment, as Top 5 IIT graduates are more likely to migrate for graduate school 
than others, and to migrate to the U.S. to attend Ph.D. programs, in particular. We find that this is 
likely due to the signaling value of the IIT brand, as well as to the networks that are formed among 
alumni of specific IIT campuses. In fact, these networks likely play an even larger role than our 
analysis of computer science faculty alumni networks suggests. 
While prior work has emphasized the gatekeeping role of elite universities in host countries 
(Kerr, et al., 2016, Amornsiripanitch, et al., 2021), our paper surfaces the similar role played by 
elite universities in source countries. While we cannot observe the full extent of the mechanisms 
contributing to these effects, our analysis suggests that through a combination of signaling and 
network effects, elite universities in source countries play a key role in shaping migration 
outcomes, both in terms of the overall propensity and the particular migration destination. We note 
19 
that the fact that elite home universities act as gatekeepers for migration further raises the stakes 
of their own admission policies. 
We conclude by mentioning two lines of inquiry that could be explored in future research. 
The first is why the incidence of migration rises dramatically at the very right tail of the talent 
distribution (above and beyond the gatekeeping role of universities). One potential explanation is 
that the private return to extraordinary ability is higher in destination countries (perhaps due to 
agglomeration effects) than in source countries. This raises the question of whether home countries 
should make special efforts to retain their top talent. It will be interesting to study whether patterns 
of migration at the right tail of the talent distribution change based on increased access to 
entrepreneurial opportunities in the home country,24 adoption of remote work, or other 
contemporary changes. The second avenue to explore is whether alternative arrangements to the 
current dominant skilled migration model (with elite universities in home and host countries acting 
as gatekeepers) would be preferable from the point of view of the source country, destination 
country, or the immigrants themselves. For instance, analysis could examine the impacts of the 
United Kingdomâs new âHigh Potential Individualâ visa route, open to graduates from the top 50 
global universities, to attract global talent.25 Other scenarios to examine in further research include 
whether IIT graduates could be hired by U.S. companies straight out of their undergraduate 
programs (instead of obtaining graduate degrees in the U.S.) or whether U.S. universities should 
formally use JEE scores in their undergraduate admission decisions or give scholarships to attract 
top talent to their institutions.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThis research paper discusses the connection between educational attainment, particularly among the exceptionally talented, and migration. 7. Conclusion 
Using a novel dataset of students taking the JEE exam and their education and career 
outcomes, we have documented that the incidence of migration among top talent is sizeable, and 
particularly so at the very right tail of the talent distribution. Among the top 1,000 scorers at the 
JEE (corresponding to 0.2% of the test takers and 0.00005% of the birth cohort), the share of 
migrants is around 36%, rising to 62% among the top 100 scorers, and to 9 out of the top 10 scorers. 
While prior literature has documented that the incidence of migration rises with educational 
attainment (Saint-Paul, 2004; Docquier and Marfouk, 2006; Docquier and Rapoport, 2012; 
Grogger and Hanson, 2011; Kerr, et al., 2016), our work reveals that this masks considerable 
heterogeneity among the tertiary-educated in the ability dimension. Indeed, the incidence of 
migration rises dramatically among the most extraordinarily able, as conjectured by Saint-Paul 
(2004). We have also documented that graduates of the most elite IITs are more likely to migrate 
abroad after graduating compared to equally talented individuals who chose other IITs. Prior 
research has documented the large enrollment of Indian students in U.S. graduate programs 
(Bound, et al., 2021). We show that Indian educational institutions are playing an important role 
in facilitating this enrollment, as Top 5 IIT graduates are more likely to migrate for graduate school 
than others, and to migrate to the U.S. to attend Ph.D. programs, in particular. We find that this is 
likely due to the signaling value of the IIT brand, as well as to the networks that are formed among 
alumni of specific IIT campuses. In fact, these networks likely play an even larger role than our 
analysis of computer science faculty alumni networks suggests.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThis part of the research focuses on the academic trajectory of 39 Indian Institute of Technology (IIT) graduates who enrolled in top-tier U.S. graduate computer science programs. Using a multinomial logit model, the researchers analyzed the influence of several factors on the enrollment decisions of these students.Key among these factors were the number of faculty members at the U.S. institution who were alumni of the IIT attended by the student, and the number of faculty members who were alumni of other IITs. The results indicate that having an alumnus from one's own IIT at a U.S. institution increases the likelihood of enrolling in that institution by 30%. In contrast, the presence of alumni from other IITs in the faculty doesn't significantly affect the enrollment decision.In conclusion, the study has found significant migration rates among top-performing students, particularly among those at the far right of the talent distribution. For example, among the top 1,000 scorers in the Joint Entrance Examination (JEE), about 36% migrate, rising to 62% among the top 100 scorers, and 90% among the top 10 scorers.The study also revealed that graduates from elite IITs have a higher likelihood of migrating after graduation compared to their equally talented peers from other IITs. The increased likelihood of migration to attend U.S. graduate programs, especially Ph.D. programs, is attributed to the reputation of the IIT brand and the networks formed among specific IIT campuses' alumni.1 / 1Trickle-down brain-drain?1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe term "trickle-down brain drain" isn't widely used or established in the literature, so I'm interpreting it based on the known concepts of "trickle-down" economics and "brain drain"."Trickle-down" economics refers to the theory that improving the financial status of society's top earners will eventually benefit poorer members of society, as the additional wealth accumulated at the top will "trickle down" to the lower economic classes.On the other hand, "brain drain" refers to the migration of highly trained or qualified individuals from their home country to another country, often in search of better living conditions, job opportunities, or wages.Combining these concepts, "trickle-down brain drain" could potentially refer to a situation where the departure of top talent from a country (the brain drain) might eventually lead to broader negative effects on society. For instance, the departure of highly skilled individuals might initially appear to impact only certain elite sectors, but over time, it could affect other sectors as well, as those sectors also rely on the skills, knowledge, and innovation of the departed individuals. This could result in reduced economic growth, lower quality education, health services, and other societal challenges.However, without a specific context or definition provided, this is only a conjectured interpretation. The phenomenon of high-achieving individuals from countries like India migrating to work in technologically advanced countries, such as the United States, is indeed often referred to as "brain drain". This migration can lead to a shortage of highly skilled individuals in the home country, potentially impacting innovation and development.However, your point about the "trickle-down" effect is also quite valid. These migrants often achieve high-level positions in their adopted countries and may contribute back to their home countries in various ways:Economic Remittances: They often send money back to their families in their home country, contributing to the local economy.Investments and Entrepreneurship: Many successful migrants invest in businesses or start their own ventures in their home countries, promoting economic growth and job creation.Transfer of Knowledge: These individuals can contribute to their home country by transferring knowledge and skills, either directly through collaborations with institutions in their home country or indirectly by mentoring fellow countrymen.Building Global Networks: By working in global companies, these individuals build networks that can benefit their home countries, for instance by facilitating partnerships between companies in both countries.Philanthropy and Social Projects: Many successful migrants contribute to social and educational projects in their home countries, thereby influencing societal development.Influence and Advocacy: These individuals often have influence in their adopted countries and can advocate for policies that benefit their home countries.That being said, the balance between the negative effects of "brain drain" and the potential positive "trickle-down" effects can vary depending on many factors, including the policies of both the home and host countries, the specific industries involved, and the individual's connections to their home country. Modernism in Australia had only reached half-mast by mid century. At this point, it was importantly still belaboured by the misconception that it meant quantifiably improved living conditions across the board. We suffered from a decadeâs lag due to our distance, but did eventually feel the Bauhaus setâs influence through a trickle-down, brain drain driven by the events of the Second World War. As Europe collapsed, figures of the âInternational Styleâ fled abroad; Mies to America, Gropius and Breuer initially to the United Kingdom. From their new vantage points in architectural practices or academic positions in the USA and UK, their vision for the Modern aesthetic was instilled in the next generation. This new guard reached Australia and New Zealand in the forms of Harry Seidler, arguably the most prominent in the former, and Ernst Plischke in the latter. In Queensland, one of our more prominent modernists was Dr Karl Langer, who moved to Brisbane in the 1930s to take up Professorship at the University of Queensland. 4 Despite increased confidence in regional styles, the country still looked desperately to Europe for approval. 5 The first ever exhibition of Australian architecture in London elicited a vicious response from The Observer when reviewing the show, succinctly stating âthere is no Australian architecture.â

Notable architects like Mies van der Rohe, Walter Gropius, and Marcel Breuer moved to countries like the US and UK, where they took up roles in architectural practices and academia. Their influence and ideas trickled down to students and other architects in these countries, and, in some cases, further abroad to places like Australia and New Zealand.In this case, the "brain drain" refers to the loss of these influential figures from Europe, while the "trickle-down" refers to the spread of their ideas and aesthetic vision through teaching, practice, and influence on others in their new locations. This led to the spread of Modernist aesthetics and architectural philosophies even to regions that were quite geographically distant from the original source, such as Australia.So while this "trickle-down, brain drain" refers to a movement of ideas and aesthetics, rather than the direct economic or technological impacts discussed earlier, there is a shared concept: the emigration of highly skilled or influential individuals can lead to the spread of their ideas or influence in their new locations, potentially benefiting these regions even as their home region experiences a 'drain' of talent.1 / 1THE WALLS

RETRO MODERN
SAM CRANSTOUN
RETRO MODERN
4 â 18 July, 2015

Nowhere is the rapid twentieth-century expansion of suburban Australia more evident than on the Gold Coast. From simple single-storey dwellings with accompanying carport and breezeblock retaining wall, to the ubiquitous blonde-brick six-pack apartment blocks, the coastâs rich identity is shaped by its architecture.  This regionally specific brand of architecture, an instantly recognisable style that both subtly and awkwardly draws from the visual language of mid-century modernism, is at the heart of Sam Cranstounâs latest exhibition, Retro Modern, at The Walls. As its starting point, this new series of sculptures borrows from the patterns of the building facades and retaining walls seen throughout the Gold Coast.  But by simplifying these patterns, reducing them to scaled-down monochromatic forms, the former glory of these designs is restored. Once associated with a local and potentially dated style of architecture, this visual style is re-examined, reconsidered. The focus is returned to the origins of this architectural style and its place within a rich history of art and design, as well as acknowledging the utopian aspirations of its modernist heritage.

Cranstounâs practice investigates how systems of representation shape a collective understanding of the past. Through the use of painting, drawing, photography, film, sculpture and installation, Cranstounâs work looks at how history is shaped by different flawed, predominantly visual, systems of representation. These investigations often revolve around different historical periods, events and figures, and incorporate research from a wide range of sources. His practice aims to explore the contemporary relevance of the monument, how the monument functions as a tool to assist memory and the roles assumed by the artist when exploring historical work, namely the artist as amateur researcher, as tourist, as fan, and as pilgrim.
_

How modernism reached Australia, went awry; or vice versa

Modernism, and especially in modernist architecture, was inherently concerned with the attainment of a pure set of forms â a utopian schema. By embracing machine production, good design would be accessible to the people, especially the working class. The roots of Modernism began with an embracing of an aesthetic informed by the factory and industry. The Deutsche Werkbund, a 1907 German collective of architects, designers and artists including Austrians Peter Behrens and Ludwig Mies van der Rohe, as well as German Walter Gropius saw the factory as a promising symbol â a site of employment and a system that championed affordability through mass manufacturing. Their first structure, the AEG factory in Berlin is a testament to this belief. This interest in the worker informed the quasi-socialist format of the Bauhaus school, set up by Gropius in Weimar, Germany in 1919, and concerned with applying contemporary and industrial materials like glass and steel to architectural contexts that provided benefit to those who were housed or employed within their structures. Gropiusâ famous adage, âform follows functionâ was born here.

Unfortunately, the Bauhaus and the aesthetic of accessibility that they promoted suffered from their own inherent contradiction and rhetoric. Entry to the Bauhaus was costly. Women were funneled away from architecture and industrial design, and into textiles and ceramics.1 Colour theory tutor Johannes Itten led a cult-like sub-group informed loosely by the tenets of Buddhism and vegetarianism.2 By the end of the 1920s Gropius gave up his directorship, initially to Marcel Breuer (designer of the iconic Wassily chair) and finally to Mies van der Rohe. In opposition to Gropius, Mies had aspirations for Bauhaus to be the pre-eminent global architecture and design school. He shifted the focus from utilitarian materials towards expensive luxuries like marble, onyx and rare tropical timbers. Though first seen in Miesâ Tugendhat House, his interest in achieving global attention and building with high quality materials coalesced in his 1929 German Pavilion for the International Exposition in Barcelona. Documented in Jeff Wallâs Morning Cleaning, Mies van der Rohe Foundation, Barcelona 1999, we see a familiar interior; a stark, unattainable luxury bastardised ad nauseam to this day on the pages of glossy magazines like House & Garden. In the background, next to a Mies-designed studded leather Barcelona chair, a no doubt lowly paid worker mops the marble floor.3

The abstraction of the modernist code through time is an interest that reappears throughout Brisbane-based artist Sam Cranstounâs work. In his 2012 exhibition, âFox River Risingâ, Cranstoun recreates in exacting graphite detail Miesâ famous Farnsworth House. Cranstoun coyly depicts the home on accentuated, almost stilt-like foundations. Portrayed as a victim of rising sea levels, in a separate work from the same exhibition, Cranstoun lifts a Barcelona chair onto four milk crates. Desperate times call for desperate measures, with the iconic chair imagined as effectively ârubbing shoulders with the massesâ, reliant upon the brawn of the âfactory workerâ as crate to lift it above the floodwaters. For âRetro Modernâ, Cranstoun has turned his attention to the modernist legacy of mid-century Queensland architecture â casting similar, subtle aspersions to the social aspirations of the post-war building projects in Brisbane and the Gold Coast.

Modernism in Australia had only reached half-mast by mid century. At this point, it was importantly still belaboured by the misconception that it meant quantifiably improved living conditions across the board. We suffered from a decadeâs lag due to our distance, but did eventually feel the Bauhaus setâs influence through a trickle-down, brain drain driven by the events of the Second World War. As Europe collapsed, figures of the âInternational Styleâ fled abroad; Mies to America, Gropius and Breuer initially to the United Kingdom. From their new vantage points in architectural practices or academic positions in the USA and UK, their vision for the Modern aesthetic was instilled in the next generation. This new guard reached Australia and New Zealand in the forms of Harry Seidler, arguably the most prominent in the former, and Ernst Plischke in the latter. In Queensland, one of our more prominent modernists was Dr Karl Langer, who moved to Brisbane in the 1930s to take up Professorship at the University of Queensland. 4 Despite increased confidence in regional styles, the country still looked desperately to Europe for approval. 5 The first ever exhibition of Australian architecture in London elicited a vicious response from The Observer when reviewing the show, succinctly stating âthere is no Australian architecture.â 6

Taking direct inspiration from âsix-packâ flats, apartments and housing in Brisbane and the Gold Coast, Cranstoun has sampled breezeblock retaining wall designs, used in brick developments in the 1950s and 60s to allow cross breezes without sacrificing the privacy of occupants. These buildings and spaces have gained viral traction in recent years as the fetishised nests of design nuts. Made with plaster and a standard brick mould, Cranstoun has rebuilt six compositions for âRetro Modernâ, installing them in close proximity to their original residential contexts. This setting allows some nice opportunities. There is a fluctuation in the weave of the bricks, from work to work, that would not be apparent in their normal occupation. An opening and closing of visibility, like lids on an eye. They are, of course, also relevant to a heritage of geometric abstraction. As an unashamed aesthete, Cranstoun does not deny the simple, visual pleasure of their form and repeated application. But this does not need also deny meaning. Flat against the wall, these patterns are without point â they no longer let air nor vision travel through. In this way, I see âRetro Modernâ as an acknowledgement of a design principle that succeeded in capturing the attention of the world, but one that has ultimately withered on the vine, waylaid by capital and aspiration. They are without function, and thus by deduction, now art.

The rhizome, a concept from Deleuze and Guattari's philosophy, represents a non-hierarchical, decentralized system where any point can be connected to any other point. This notion can be contrasted with tree-like structures that have a clear hierarchy and center.Applying this to urbanization and migration, people moving from rural areas to urban centers, or from one country to another, form a kind of rhizomatic network. They maintain connections with their places of origin, and also form new connections in their new locations. These connections can lead to a wide range of effects, including economic benefits, the spread of ideas and innovation, and the development of multicultural communities.The pace of urbanization indeed continues to accelerate, particularly in developing countries. This brings new opportunities, but also challenges. Understanding these processes through the lens of a rhizomatic network can provide valuable insights, particularly as technology increasingly enables more complex, global connections.1 / 1End an essay on these topics and the following ones, titled "Trickle-down Network Effects":

161 pages1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn this complex, interconnected world, the concept of "Trickle-Down Network Effects" is increasingly becoming a dominant factor shaping our societies and economies. Stemming from a variety of disciplines and practices, the idea offers us a new perspective to examine our contemporary realities.In the context of education and migration, we've seen how the reputational magnetism of elite institutions like the Indian Institutes of Technology (IITs) in India or renowned architecture schools in Europe can instigate a trickle-down effect. These institutions serve as launching pads for the talents they foster, leading to a subsequent brain drain effect, which can alter regional development dynamics.Shifting our focus to the rapidly urbanizing world, we see similar patterns emerging. The global trend of urbanization has shown rhizomatic network effects, where the movement of people, ideas, and resources are interconnected in intricate and non-hierarchical ways. Cities like Montreal, Toronto, and Vancouver in Canada demonstrate these effects, where they draw in individuals from less populous regions, shaping the economic, social, and cultural landscapes of both the origin and destination locations.The idea of trickle-down network effects also resonates in the digital realm. Concepts such as the 'trickle-down profiler' in computing represent a form of this phenomenon, where network settings disseminate from a central router to the connected machines.Our world is one of networks, both physical and virtual, shaped by flows of people, resources, and information. The impacts of these flows are not uniform but trickle down in ways that create disparities and opportunities, new challenges, and possibilities. These networks permeate various sectors â from wine distribution during a pandemic to the accessibility of Internet services.As we continue to navigate this ever-evolving landscape, the understanding of these "Trickle-Down Network Effects" will provide valuable insights into shaping more resilient, inclusive, and adaptive societies. In our quest for more equitable futures, we must learn to better leverage these network effects, ensuring that the trickling down isn't just a one-way flow, but creates a feedback loop that can uplift all within the network. This notion will bring us closer to achieving a world where opportunity and prosperity are not just exclusive to specific nodes but are shared characteristics of the entire network.Ultimately, the value in recognizing the concept of "Trickle-Down Network Effects" lies in the possibility of reframing our view of globalization, migration, education, urbanization, and technology. It serves as a reminder that our world is interconnected and interdependent in ways that demand our understanding and compassionate engagement.1 / 1Trickle-up fashion

On the Catwalk (42120103371)
The trickle-up effect in the fashion field, also known as bubble-up pattern, is an innovative fashion theory, born in the late 1970s, that believes that new trends are to be found in the streets and that innovation flows from lower classes to upper ones.[1] It is completely in contrast with classical theories of fashion consumption, such as the ones of Georg Simmel and Thorstein Veblen, according to which the upper classes are the ones who dictate the fashion flow.[2]

Origins of the trickle-down and trickle-up effects
The trickle-down effect can be seen as an antithesis to the trickle-up effect. Although the theory itself has only appeared since the 1950s under the title trickle-down effect, the concept can be traced back to Georg Simmel and Thorstein Veblen.[citation needed] It describes the inability of lower social classes to develop a fashion style of their own, as only upper social classes have the possibility to influence fashion. Lower social classes are therefore dependent on imitating the fashion of the rich.[2] In contrast to this, the concept of the trickle-up effect describes an upward diffusion in which fashion styles from lower classes are adopted by upper ones.[2] In opposition to the diffusion of fashion in earlier years, we now encounter a phenomenon in which social strata that are primarily considered to be of lower social standing influence the fashion taste of the time.[citation needed] As the trickle-up effect becomes more and more influential for todayâs fashion consumption, it is necessary to mention that it was firstly described by Paul Blumberg during the 1970s in the USA: "[â¦] there has been in the last decade more percolating up from the bottom than trickling down from the top".[1] Blumberg elaborates on this by stating that a variety of standards in fashion have been set by the dÃ©classÃ© and anti-class youth by using new styles, like long hair or a shabby chic, to not only tease the status symbols of higher classes but also spreading their styles into the fashion elite.[1]1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe "Trickle-Up Effect" in fashion, also known as the "Bubble-Up Pattern," proposes that new trends originate in the streets and progress from lower to upper social classes. This theory, which emerged in the late 1970s, upends the classical understanding of fashion consumption. Theorists like Georg Simmel and Thorstein Veblen posited that the upper classes dictate fashion trends, with lower classes following suit. The trickle-up effect contradicts this view, suggesting that innovation often stems from grassroots level.Historically, many fashion trends have followed this upward trajectory. T-shirts and jeans, for instance, originated as functional garments for working-class people and gradually became wardrobe staples for all social strata. Similarly, the punk style born out of rebellion against societal norms influenced mainstream fashion and was adopted by fashion elites. The same pattern is seen in the adoption of military-inspired clothing, hippie fashion from the 70s, yuppie fashion of the 80s, and the grunge influence in the 90s.The rise of digital culture and social media has further bolstered the trickle-up effect in fashion. Platforms like Instagram have democratized the fashion industry, enabling individuals to influence trends irrespective of their social standing. Streetwear, once considered fringe or subcultural, has gained mainstream acceptance, thanks to the role of influencers and online fashion communities.In summary, the Trickle-Up Effect represents an essential dynamic in the world of fashion, demonstrating how innovation can percolate upwards from the streets to the runways. This effect is a testament to the democratization of fashion and the power of individual expression in shaping trends, regardless of social class. It's a fascinating counterpoint to the traditional top-down view of fashion, offering an egalitarian perspective where anyone can be a trendsetter.1 / 1I heard that hundreds of years ago, the Arabic sheiks had developed a system non-proscriptive grammar. Say a dispute over boundaries or property rights came down to a dispute over a certain term. Rather than consult the experts, they would send an expedition to find some Bedouin or camel herder and try to elicit the term in conversation. Their use could trump the opinion of the grammarians and what was written in the books, because it was considered that children  and wandering tent dwellers had been less corrupted by intellectual pursuits, and in away were closer to pure language. This was similar to the greek notion of aleuthis (knowledge and truth as unforgetting). I thought this might be a kind of trickle-up effect on grammar.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIndeed, the historical example you've provided can certainly be construed as a form of trickle-up influence, albeit in the realm of language and linguistics rather than fashion or social trends.It's a fascinating example of how society can look to "grassroots" sources for knowledge and understanding, in this case, to resolve ambiguities in language or legal disputes. These Bedouin camel herders, unspoiled by the intellectual pursuit of grammar, were seen as preserving the 'purity' of the language, thus having a vital role in clarifying language use and potentially influencing linguistic norms.This approach is not too different from how the 'trickle-up' effect operates in fashion, technology, or culture, where innovation and trends often originate at the grassroots level and then rise to broader societal recognition and acceptance.Your reference to the Greek notion of "aletheia" â knowledge and truth as an act of un-forgetting, or the disclosure of what was previously hidden â also lends an interesting philosophical layer to this discussion. It illustrates the pursuit of authenticity and the belief that wisdom can be gleaned from the seemingly overlooked or marginalized sections of society.In conclusion, this phenomenon of referencing 'pure' or 'authentic' sources to guide societal practices is indeed a form of the trickle-up effect. Whether in fashion, technology, cultural practices, or language norms, this pattern signifies the potential for innovation and truth to emerge from anywhere within society, not solely from the top echelons.1 / 1Can you give some examples?1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTSure, here are some examples of trickle-up effects in various fields:Fashion: As mentioned earlier, street fashion often serves as a source of inspiration for high-end designers. One example of this is the adoption of skateboard culture in the mainstream fashion industry. Brands like Supreme and Palace began as local skate brands but are now considered high fashion, worn by celebrities and sought after by consumers worldwide.Technology: Open-source software is a great example of trickle-up in technology. Originally developed by individual programmers and hobbyist communities, open-source platforms and software like Linux, WordPress, or Apache, have become industry standards, even in large corporations and government organizations.Music: Many musical genres and trends start in small, local communities before being adopted by mainstream artists and record companies. Hip-hop and punk rock, for instance, started as countercultural movements before becoming globally popular music genres.Slang and Language: Much like your example of Bedouin camel herders influencing Arabic grammar, in many languages, slang or colloquial terms that originate in subcultures or regional dialects often make their way into mainstream language use. Internet slang is a prime example of this. Terms like "LOL," "BRB," "selfie," and "meme" started in niche online communities and have now entered everyday vocabulary and even formal dictionaries.Food and Cuisine: Street food and home cooking from various cultures often influence haute cuisine. For instance, many gourmet restaurants now offer high-end versions of traditionally low-brow dishes, like burgers, tacos, or ramen.Environmental and Social Movements: Movements like organic farming, fair trade, and recycling were initially embraced by small groups of environmentally conscious individuals. These practices have now been adopted by larger corporations and are considered mainstream.These examples highlight how ideas and trends can percolate up from grassroots or subcultures and influence mainstream society, demonstrating the broad scope and impact of the trickle-up effect.1 / 1And at the same time, the hoi poi seek to emulate the fashion and taste of the elite, say royalty, billionaires, and celebrities.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTAbsolutely, you're describing what's traditionally known as the "trickle-down effect" in sociology and economics. It refers to the phenomenon where trends, styles, and behaviors are initiated by the upper classes and then adopted by lower classes. Here are some examples:Fashion: For a long time, fashion has been dictated by what royalty and the elite wear. For instance, during the reign of King Louis XIV in France, his court and their opulent styles were emulated by the bourgeoisie and later the working class. More recently, "fast fashion" retailers quickly produce clothing that mirrors what's seen on high-fashion runways, enabling the masses to wear trends initiated by high-end designers.Technology: When a new gadget or technology hits the market, it's often expensive and only accessible to the wealthy. Over time, these products become more affordable and widely available to everyone. The smartphone is a perfect example of this; initially, only those who could afford the high price tag owned one, but now they're ubiquitous.Lifestyle and Habits: Often, the lifestyle and habits of the rich and famous become aspirational for the general population. This can be seen in the popularity of wellness and fitness trends, luxury goods, travel destinations, and even certain diets.Arts and Culture: In many societies, forms of art, music, and literature that were initially patronized by the elites gradually gain popularity among the masses. Opera and ballet, for instance, were historically associated with the aristocracy but have become more accessible to wider audiences.Architectural Styles: Architectural styles and interior design trends often trickle down from elite circles. For instance, the minimalist, industrial aesthetic was popularized by high-end designers and architects before it became mainstream.In this way, both the trickle-down and trickle-up effects are at play in society, influencing how trends and ideas spread across different social strata.1 / 1Is "brain drain" a myth? Politicians and business leaders over the last decade have referenced the âbrain drainâ as a major problem for the state of Michigan. College students graduate from a state college or university and move elsewhere to pursue a job or begin their career.

Some say this âbrain drainâ is because there arenât enough opportunities in Michigan, or itâs not a desirable place to live. Or maybe young people just want to spread their wings? Regardless of the reason, our elected officials have used it as a talking point. Gov. Rick Snyder has referenced it numerous times during his time in office, and weâve even seen legislation introduced to provide tax credits to slow down the talent exodus.
 

The thing that has made Michigan a great state isn't all this baloney about industrial recruitment and keeping companies and bailing out industries, it's because you have great state-funded institutions ... and the state should recommit to that
  Thereâs always going to be graduates leaving the state, but is that exodus as massive as we think?

An in-depth new study from Jonathan Rothwell at the Brookings Institutionâs Metropolitan Policy Program shows that the brain drain trend is reversing. The study shows the market retaining the most college graduates in the country is actually Metro Detroit.

According to the study, 77.7% of college grads in Metro Detroit stay in the area after they get their degree. Even if you only count four-year institutions, the Motor City sits at 70.2%, which is third-best in the nation.

Richard Florida wrote about the study for The Atlantic and joined Stateside to talk about the data and says politicians have it all wrong about what makes a strong state economy.

âThe thing that has made Michigan a great state isnât all this baloney about industrial recruitment and keeping companies and bailing out industries,â said Florida. âItâs because you have great state-funded institutions like the University of Michigan and Michigan State and Wayne State. And the state should recommit to that.â

Florida cites a variety of examples of economic successes around the country like in California, Texas and North Carolina. They all have one thing in common: great state university systems.
 

I think what's happening is that Detroit is coming back on its own
  What else has led to the increase in talent retention in Metro Detroit?

âI think whatâs happening is that Detroit is coming back on its own,â said Florida. âNot because of any federal policy, not because of a government bailout, people are fed up paying the prices of New York and San Francisco where, for a million dollars you canât get a shoebox to live in. So itâs an opportunity for [people to live in] â¦ a cool, interesting, gritty, urban, sophisticated, cosmopolitan city just like Detroit.

"Itâs a moment," he added. "All of the things that have been going on there make it much more attractive. Iâm talking to people all over the country who are thinking about Detroit, moving to Detroit, excited about Detroit. So I think thatâs whatâs happening. Detroitâs moment is now.â

Florida said when you expand the data to include Ann Arbor and Lansing, the numbers are still good. Just not Top 5 in the nation level good. He said they fall at about 57% for all colleges and that number drops to about 40% for just four-year institutions.

Josh Hakala, a lifelong Michigander (East Lansing & Edwardsburg), comes to Michigan Radio after nearly two decades of working in a variety of fields within broadcasting and digital media.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThis is indeed an interesting spin on the "brain drain" narrative. According to the study referenced in the article, rather than Michigan suffering a significant exodus of college-educated individuals, the state, and especially the Metro Detroit area, is actually retaining a substantial percentage of its college graduates. This suggests that the supposed "brain drain" might not be as severe as previously thought, at least not in this area.The "brain drain" theory often posits that college graduates from a certain region or country tend to move to other places with better opportunities for jobs or career advancement. This has been a concern in many places, as losing educated individuals can hamper local economic development and innovation.This study suggests that such a drain is not happening to the extent previously thought in Michigan, with the majority of college graduates in Metro Detroit staying in the area after graduation. Richard Florida, a scholar who wrote about the study, attributes this in part to the revival of Detroit as a desirable place to live and work, along with the strength of Michigan's state-funded institutions like the University of Michigan and Michigan State.However, it is also important to note that different regions and countries may have different experiences regarding "brain drain." While Michigan might be successfully retaining its graduates, other places might still be struggling with the departure of educated individuals.Thus, while this study provides valuable insights into graduate retention in Michigan, it does not necessarily debunk the broader concept of "brain drain." The experiences of Michigan might provide lessons for other regions and countries on how to retain their educated workforce, such as the importance of investment in local institutions and making the area an attractive place to live and work.1 / 1JHA, AKHILESHWAR. 22(23) 16 Dec. 78, p.32. 1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTI'm afraid I don't have direct access to the specific articles you've referenced, as they are not included in my training data and I don't have the ability to look up new information or access databases in real-time.However, I can tell you that the concept of "brain drain" has been a topic of significant debate in the academic and policy communities. While some argue that the migration of highly skilled individuals from developing to developed countries (or from one region to another within a country) can have negative impacts on the source regions or countries, others suggest that this is not necessarily the case.Some research suggests that "brain drain" can actually have positive effects, especially when you consider the potential for "brain gain" or "brain circulation." For example, individuals who migrate for education or work opportunities can acquire new skills and experiences, create international networks, and then contribute to their home countries or regions if they return or through remittances, investments, or knowledge transfers while they are abroad. This is sometimes referred to as "reverse flow of aid."Efforts to reverse the brain drain process, as mentioned in Nitish S. Rele's article, typically involve measures to attract highly skilled individuals back to their home countries or regions, or to create conditions that encourage them to stay in the first place. This could involve improving educational and career opportunities, creating a conducive environment for entrepreneurship and innovation, and addressing quality of life issues, among other strategies.The issue of brain drain and efforts to address it can have significant implications for economic development, social equity, and international relations. The specific impacts and effectiveness of these efforts can depend on a wide range of factors, including the specific characteristics of the individuals involved, the conditions in the source and destination countries or regions, and the nature of the broader global economic and political context.It would be interesting to see how these issues are discussed in the articles you mentioned, as they could provide more specific insights into the dynamics of brain drain and strategies to address it in specific contexts.1 / 1This "reverse flow of aid" was what I meant earlier by "trickle-down brain-drain"1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTAh, I see what you're saying. The term "trickle-down brain drain" isn't a common term, but your use of it makes sense in this context.In your usage, "trickle-down brain drain" seems to refer to the notion that despite the outward migration of educated individuals (brain drain), there can be a "trickling down" of benefits to their home countries or regions. These benefits can be in the form of remittances, the creation of business networks, the transfer of knowledge and skills, or contributions to the home country if the individuals return. In other words, while brain drain could be seen as a loss for the source region or country, there might also be a compensatory or even beneficial effect.This phenomenon can indeed be viewed as a kind of "reverse flow of aid" or "brain gain." It's a complex process and can depend on many factors, such as the policies of the home and host countries, the skills of the migrants, and their connections to their home countries.However, it's also worth noting that these benefits do not automatically occur in all cases of brain drain, and the net impact can be hard to measure. In many instances, the loss of human capital due to brain drain can have significant negative impacts, particularly for developing countries or disadvantaged regions within developed countries. Balancing these dynamics is a key challenge for policymakers.1 / 1Anything else you would like to add?1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTCertainly. The topic of "brain drain," and its potential reverse effects, is complex and multi-faceted. Here are a few additional points that can expand on this:Diaspora Networks: Individuals who migrate for education or work often establish strong networks in their host countries. These networks can facilitate trade, investment, and knowledge exchange between their host and home countries.The Role of Technology: With advancements in communication technologies, it's easier than ever for individuals living abroad to contribute to their home countries. They can remotely participate in business ventures, provide consultations, and even teach or mentor, effectively reversing some of the effects of brain drain.Brain Circulation: Rather than a one-way movement, migration is increasingly seen as circular. Educated professionals may leave their home countries to gain experience abroad, but they might return home after a period, bringing with them valuable skills, experiences, and networks. This is often referred to as "brain circulation."Policy Considerations: Policies play a crucial role in determining whether brain drain can have compensating benefits. For instance, policies that facilitate remittances, encourage return migration, or support the involvement of the diaspora in home country affairs can help turn brain drain into brain gain.The Need for Further Research: While there's increasing recognition of the potential benefits of brain drain, it's also clear that these benefits aren't automatic. There's a need for further research to understand the conditions under which brain drain can lead to brain gain, and how policies can maximize these benefits while minimizing the drawbacks.Remember that while there can be some potential benefits stemming from the brain drain phenomenon, it's not without its challenges and negative impacts, especially for regions that heavily rely on the expertise and skills of the individuals leaving. The real challenge lies in creating the right conditions that encourage not just the outflow, but also the return and fruitful engagement of talent.1 / 1Migration Policy Institute

Brain Drain & Brain Gain
_ImmigrantBusinessPeople
The movement of skilled workers internationally represents brain gain for the countries that reap their skills and experience and brain drain for their countries of origin. On the brain gain side of the divide, countries increasingly are looking to position their immigration policies to attract the types of international workers and students whose skills they desire. On the brain drain side, the development impacts of losing educated workers are being assessed in immigrant-sending and receiving countries alike as the research presented here shows.

Born This Way: Chomskyâs Theory Explains Why Weâre So Good at Acquiring Language
Medically reviewed by Timothy J. Legg, PhD, PsyD â By Rebecca Joy Stanborough, MFA on June 14, 2019
Innate skill
Universal grammar
Linguists debate
In classrooms
Bottom line
Humans are storytelling beings. As far as we know, no other species has the capacity for language and ability to use it in endlessly creative ways. From our earliest days, we name and describe things. We tell others whatâs happening around us.

For people immersed in the study of language and the study of learning, one really important question has engendered a lot of debate over the years: How much of this ability is innate â part of our genetic makeup â and how much do we learn from our environments?


An innate capacity for language
Thereâs no doubt that we acquire our native languages, complete with their vocabularies and grammatical patterns.

But is there an inherited ability underlying our individual languages â a structural framework that enables us to grasp, retain, and develop language so easily?

In 1957, linguist Noam Chomsky published a groundbreaking book called âSyntactic Structures.â It proposed a novel idea: All human beings may be born with an innate understanding of how language works.

Whether we learn Arabic, English, Chinese, or sign language is determined, of course, by the circumstances of our lives.

But according to Chomsky, we can acquire language because weâre genetically encoded with a universal grammar â a basic understanding of how communication is structured.

Chomskyâs idea has since become widely accepted.

What convinced Chomsky that a universal grammar exists?
Languages share certain basic traits
Chomsky and other linguists have said that all languages contain similar elements. For example, globally speaking, language breaks down into similar categories of words: nouns, verbs, and adjectives, to name three.

Another shared characteristic of language is recursionTrusted Source. With rare exceptions, all languages use structures that repeat themselves, allowing us to expand those structures almost infinitely.

For example, take the structure of a descriptor. In almost every known language, itâs possible to repeat descriptors over and over again: âShe wore an itsy-bitsy, teeny-weeny, yellow polka dot bikini.â

Strictly speaking, more adjectives could be added to further describe that bikini, each embedded within the existing structure.

The recursive property of language allows us to expand the sentence âShe believed Ricky was innocentâ almost endlessly: âLucy believed that Fred and Ethel knew Ricky had insisted he was innocent.â

The recursive property of language is sometimes called ânesting,â because in almost all languages, sentences can be expanded by placing repeating structures inside each other.

Chomsky and others have argued that because almost all languages share these characteristics despite their other variations, we may be born preprogrammed with a universal grammar.

We learn language almost effortlessly
Linguists like Chomsky have argued for a universal grammar in part because children everywhere develop language in very similar ways in short periods of time with little assistance.

Children show awareness of language categories at extremely early ages, long before any overt instruction occurs.

For example, one study showed that 18-month-old children recognized âa dokeâ referred to a thing and âprachingâ referred to an action, showing they understood the form of the word.

Having the article âaâ before it or ending with â-ingâ determined whether the word was an object or an event.

Itâs possible they had learned these ideas from listening to people talk, but those who espouse the idea of a universal grammar say itâs more likely that they have an innate understanding of how words function, even if they donât know the words themselves.

And we learn in the same sequence
Proponents of universal grammar say children the world over naturally develop language in the same sequence of steps.

So, what does that shared developmental pattern look like? Many linguists agree that there are three basic stages:

learning sounds
learning words
learning sentences
More specifically:

We perceive and produce speech sounds.
We babble, usually with a consonant-then-vowel pattern.
We speak our first rudimentary words.
We grow our vocabularies, learning to classify things.
We build two-word sentences, and then increase the complexity of our sentences.
Different children proceed through these stages at different rates. But the fact that we all share the same developmental sequence may show weâre hardwired for language.

We learn despite a âpoverty of stimulusâ
Chomsky and others have also argued that we learn complex languages, with their intricate grammatical rules and limitations, without receiving explicit instruction.

For example, children automatically grasp the correct way to arrange dependent sentence structures without being taught.

We know to say âThe boy who is swimming wants to eat lunchâ instead of âThe boy wants to eat lunch who is swimming.â

Despite this lack of instructional stimulus, we still learn and use our native languages, understanding the rules that govern them. We wind up knowing a lot more about how our languages work than weâre ever overtly taught.


Linguists love a good debate
Noam Chomsky is among the most oft-quoted linguists in history. Nevertheless, thereâs been lots of debate around his universal grammar theory for over half a century now.

One fundamental argument is that heâs got it wrong about a biological framework for language acquisition. Linguists and educators who differ with him say we acquire language the same way we learn everything else: through our exposure to stimuli in our environment.

Our parents speak to us, whether verbally or using signs. We âabsorbâ language by listening to conversations taking place all around us, from the subtle corrections we receive for our linguistic errors.

For instance, a child says, âI no want that.â

Their caregiver responds, âYou mean, âI donât want that.ââ

But Chomskyâs theory of universal grammar doesnât deal with how we learn our native languages. Itâs focused on the innate capacity that makes all our language learning possible.

A more fundamental criticismTrusted Source is that there are hardly any properties shared by all languages.

Take recursion, for example. There are languages that simply arenât recursive.

And if the principles and parameters of language arenât really universal, how could there be an underlying âgrammarâ programmed into our brains?

So, how does this theory affect language learning in classrooms?
One of the most practical outgrowths has been the idea that thereâs an optimal age for language acquisition among children.

The younger, the better is the prevailing idea. Since young children are primed for natural language acquisition, learning a second language may be more effective in early childhood.

The universal grammar theory has also had a profound influence on classrooms where students are learning second languages.

Many teachers now use more natural, immersive approaches that mimic the way we acquire our first languages, rather than memorizing grammatical rules and vocabulary lists.

Teachers who understand universal grammar may also be better prepared to explicitly focus on the structural differences between studentsâ first and second languages.


The bottom line
Noam Chomskyâs theory of universal grammar says that weâre all born with an innate understanding of the way language works.

Chomsky based his theory on the idea that all languages contain similar structures and rules (a universal grammar), and the fact that children everywhere acquire language the same way, and without much effort, seems to indicate that weâre born wired with the basics already present in our brains.

Although not everyone agrees with Chomskyâs theory, it continues to have a profound influence on how we think about language acquisition today.


Last medically reviewed on June 14, 2019

The printed sheets were taken from the

of hands, each adding his or her share to  
the necessary work of completing the  
magazine.  
The covers, which are printed on both  
sides of a double sheet, thus giving two  
covers to each" sheet, were cut in half on  
the âDiamondâ cutting machine, and then  
taken away to be folded by hand in lots of  
twelve, ready to receive the inside sheets.  
These inside sheets contain 16 pages, eight  
pages being printed on either side, and the  
process of folding and insetting bring the  
folios into consecutive order, from 1 to 40.  
An interesting point in the folding and in-  
setting of the sheets is the order of the  
page numbers when they leave the machine.  
One sheet on the front side would be num-  
bered as follows:âs, 8,9, 12, 29, 32, 33,  
36. On the other side, the numbers would  
be 6,7, 10, 11, 30, 31, 34, and 35. When  
folded, these numbers would run from 5 to  
12 and then from 29 to 36, leaving a space  
to inset folded sheets with numbers run-

sheets would then be inset in a folded sheet  
numbered as follows:â1, 2,3, 4, 37, 38,  
39 and 40. The whole would then be placed  
into the folded cover, thus making a com-  
plete magazine ready for stitching and  
trimming.

by the âEclipseâ Folding Machine, which  
is constructed for fast running on magazine  
The Payne "Diamondâ Cutting Machine.  
work. There is nothing like the same de-

the correct position in the folding machine,  
it is automatically drawn into the cor-  
rect position for each fold. Each sheet is  
folded three times, and so the double sheet  
is reduced to the one page size. The first  
fold is obtained by the feeder taking a  
sheet from the stack at hand on the feed  
board and placing it in the machine under  
a drop roll. This roll, coming into contact  
with the sheet, gives it an impetus which  
carries it through into a series of long  
tapes which hold the sheet to the first

sheet broadside. While the sheet is held  
in that position by the tapes, a sidearm  
with a pair of jaws pushes it along about  
one inch and then the jaws close and draw  
the sheet back to a given spot. A large  
blade descends at this moment and tucks  
the sheet between two steel rollers, which  
give it the first fold. The second and  
third folds to the 16-page sheet are carried  
out in a similar manner, but on different  
levels of the machine, and on a smaller  
scale, until the sheet is reduced to folded  
size required.  
The machine folds at the rate of 2,000  
sheets per hour, where the most expert  
worker, folding a sheet by hand, could  
not average more than 250 per hour.

The cutting machine has incorporated in  
it many good points, which make a strong  
appeal where accuracy' and getatableness  
are necessary. This machine automatically  
holds or, technically, clamps the magazines  
in position while the knife descends and  
trims them, and the clamp releases its grip  
and runs up out of the way when the knife  
has done its work, leaving the operator  
plenty of room to handle the material that  
is going through the cutting process. So  
that much of the laborious work of the cut-  
ting machine operator is eliminated.  
When the cutting machine is finished  
with the magazines, they are taken to the  
publishing room to be addressed and des-  
patched to the numerous newsagents and  
subscribers.  
The addressing is done by a stencilling  
process on a machine which prints the name  
and address of the subscriber on the wrap-  
per before âThe Lone Handâ is wrapped.  
Attached to this machine is a long upright;  
tube or chamber, in which the address  
stencils are held, always ready for use. The  
stencils are released from the chamber one  
by one, until every wrapper has been ad-  
dressd. An attachment pushes the bottom  
stencil to the centres of the machine, where  
the wrapper is held in position. A heavily  
inked roller passes over the face of the  
stencil, forcing the ink through the perfor-  
ated formation of the address on to the  
wrapper. The second stencil follows on,  
pushing the first one out of the way into a  
chamber on the other side of the machine.  
And so the process is continued until the  
whole job is finished, and the stencils are  
left ready to be used over again next  
month.  
For addresses for new subscribers fresh  
stencils have to be made, and these are  
produced on a special typewriter, containing  
sharp points instead of letters, as in the  
case of ordinary typewriters. These points  
perforate the parchment, of which the sten-  

cils are made, thus forming the name and  
address required.
